<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Flood</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Water overflow</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Flood_(disambiguation)" title="Flood (disambiguation)">Flood (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urban_flood_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="427" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Urban_flood_cropped.jpg/220px-Urban_flood_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Urban_flood_cropped.jpg/330px-Urban_flood_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/36/Urban_flood_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urban_flood_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flooding in a street in <a href="/wiki/Morpeth,_Northumberland" title="Morpeth, Northumberland">Morpeth</a>, England. Flooding is increasing with <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">extreme weather</a> events caused by climate change are creating rainfall events with much more rain than in the past. Cities and towns built on waterbodies or with infrastructure designed around historical rainfall patterns are increasingly susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Urban_flooding" title="Urban flooding">urban flooding</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A <b>flood</b> is an overflow of water (<a href="/wiki/List_of_non-water_floods" title="List of non-water floods">or rarely other fluids</a>) that submerges land that is usually dry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In the sense of "flowing water", the word may also be applied to the inflow of the <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tide</a>. Floods are an area of study of the discipline <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrology</a> and are of significant concern in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineering" title="Civil engineering">civil engineering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a>.   <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues" title="Environmental issues">Human changes to the environment</a> often increase the intensity and frequency of flooding, for example land use changes such as <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wetland_conservation" title="Wetland conservation">removal of wetlands</a>, changes in waterway course or <a href="/wiki/Flood_control" title="Flood control">flood controls</a> such as with <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levees</a>, and larger environmental issues such as <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">sea level rise</a>. In particular climate change's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_weather" title="Effects of climate change on weather">increased rainfall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">extreme weather events</a> increases the severity of other causes for flooding, resulting in more intense floods and increased flood risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Flooding may occur as an overflow of water from water bodies, such as a <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lake</a>, or ocean, in which the water overtops or breaks <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levees</a>, resulting in some of that water escaping its usual boundaries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> or it may occur due to an accumulation of rainwater on saturated ground in an areal flood. While the size of a lake or other body of water will vary with seasonal changes in <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> and snow melt, these changes in size are unlikely to be considered significant unless they flood <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property</a> or <a href="/wiki/Drowning" title="Drowning">drown</a> <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_animals" title="Domestication of animals">domestic animals</a>.
</p><p>Floods can also occur in rivers when the flow rate exceeds the capacity of the <a href="/wiki/Channel_(geography)" title="Channel (geography)">river channel</a>, particularly at bends or <a href="/wiki/Meander" title="Meander">meanders</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Waterway" title="Waterway">waterway</a>. Floods often cause damage to homes and businesses if they are in the natural flood plains of rivers.  While riverine flood damage can be eliminated by moving away from rivers and other bodies of water, people have traditionally lived and worked by rivers because the land is usually flat and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_(soil)" title="Fertility (soil)">fertile</a> and because rivers provide easy travel and access to commerce and industry. Flooding can lead to secondary consequences in addition to damage to property, such as long-term displacement of residents and creating increased spread of <a href="/wiki/Waterborne_diseases" title="Waterborne diseases">waterborne diseases</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">vector-bourne disesases</a> transmitted by mosquitos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Erschrecklichewasserfluth.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="752" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Erschrecklichewasserfluth.jpg/220px-Erschrecklichewasserfluth.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Erschrecklichewasserfluth.jpg/330px-Erschrecklichewasserfluth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Erschrecklichewasserfluth.jpg/440px-Erschrecklichewasserfluth.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Erschrecklichewasserfluth.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary picture of the flood that struck the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> coast of <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> in October 1634.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Flood_on_Java_1865-1876_Raden_Saleh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="893" data-file-width="1241" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/A_Flood_on_Java_1865-1876_Raden_Saleh.jpg/220px-A_Flood_on_Java_1865-1876_Raden_Saleh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/A_Flood_on_Java_1865-1876_Raden_Saleh.jpg/330px-A_Flood_on_Java_1865-1876_Raden_Saleh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/A_Flood_on_Java_1865-1876_Raden_Saleh.jpg/440px-A_Flood_on_Java_1865-1876_Raden_Saleh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Flood_on_Java_1865-1876_Raden_Saleh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>People seeking refuge from flood in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jawa_Tengah" title="Jawa Tengah">Jawa Tengah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>. ca. 1865–1876.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Katrina-new-orleans-flooding3-2005.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Katrina-new-orleans-flooding3-2005.jpg/220px-Katrina-new-orleans-flooding3-2005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Katrina-new-orleans-flooding3-2005.jpg/330px-Katrina-new-orleans-flooding3-2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Katrina-new-orleans-flooding3-2005.jpg/440px-Katrina-new-orleans-flooding3-2005.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Katrina-new-orleans-flooding3-2005.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of flooded New Orleans in the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>. New Orleans has historically been vulnerable to flooding because it is on a river delta and experiences hurricanes. Katrina's extreme rainfall and poor infrastructure maintenance led to <a href="/wiki/Levee_breach" title="Levee breach">a levee breach</a> which flooded large portions of the city.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venezia-Venice-Venedig-at-night_JBU-02.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3075" data-file-width="4612" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Venezia-Venice-Venedig-at-night_JBU-02.JPG/220px-Venezia-Venice-Venedig-at-night_JBU-02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Venezia-Venice-Venedig-at-night_JBU-02.JPG/330px-Venezia-Venice-Venedig-at-night_JBU-02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Venezia-Venice-Venedig-at-night_JBU-02.JPG/440px-Venezia-Venice-Venedig-at-night_JBU-02.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venezia-Venice-Venedig-at-night_JBU-02.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Regular" flooding in <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rapid_Creek_flooding_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="997" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Rapid_Creek_flooding_1.jpg/220px-Rapid_Creek_flooding_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Rapid_Creek_flooding_1.jpg/330px-Rapid_Creek_flooding_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Rapid_Creek_flooding_1.jpg/440px-Rapid_Creek_flooding_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rapid_Creek_flooding_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flooding of a creek due to heavy <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoonal</a> rain and high tide in <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">Darwin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jeddah_Flood_-_King_Abdullah_Street.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jeddah_Flood_-_King_Abdullah_Street.jpg/220px-Jeddah_Flood_-_King_Abdullah_Street.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jeddah_Flood_-_King_Abdullah_Street.jpg/330px-Jeddah_Flood_-_King_Abdullah_Street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jeddah_Flood_-_King_Abdullah_Street.jpg/440px-Jeddah_Flood_-_King_Abdullah_Street.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jeddah_Flood_-_King_Abdullah_Street.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flood in <a href="/wiki/Jeddah" title="Jeddah">Jeddah</a>, covering the King Abdullah Street in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Areal">Areal</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:House_surrounded_by_flood_Ilmajoki_Finland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2334" data-file-width="3156" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/House_surrounded_by_flood_Ilmajoki_Finland.jpg/220px-House_surrounded_by_flood_Ilmajoki_Finland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/House_surrounded_by_flood_Ilmajoki_Finland.jpg/330px-House_surrounded_by_flood_Ilmajoki_Finland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/House_surrounded_by_flood_Ilmajoki_Finland.jpg/440px-House_surrounded_by_flood_Ilmajoki_Finland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:House_surrounded_by_flood_Ilmajoki_Finland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In spring time, the floods are quite typical in <a href="/wiki/Ostrobothnia_(historical_province)" title="Ostrobothnia (historical province)">Ostrobothnia</a>, a flat-lying area in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>. A flood-surrounded house in <a href="/wiki/Ilmajoki" title="Ilmajoki">Ilmajoki</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Ostrobothnia" title="South Ostrobothnia">South Ostrobothnia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Floods can happen on flat or low-lying areas when water is supplied by rainfall or snowmelt more rapidly than it can either <a href="/wiki/Infiltration_(hydrology)" title="Infiltration (hydrology)">infiltrate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">run off</a>. The excess accumulates in place, sometimes to hazardous depths. Surface <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> can become saturated, which effectively stops infiltration, where the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a> is shallow, such as a <a href="/wiki/Floodplain" title="Floodplain">floodplain</a>, or from intense rain from one or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storm_train" title="Storm train">series of storms</a>. Infiltration also is slow to negligible through frozen ground, rock, <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a>, paving, or roofs. Areal flooding begins in flat areas like floodplains and in local depressions not connected to a stream channel, because the velocity of <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">overland flow</a> depends on the surface slope. <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">Endorheic basins</a> may experience areal flooding during periods when precipitation exceeds evaporation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Riverine_.28Channel.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Riverine_(Channel)">Riverine (Channel)</span></h3>
<p>Floods occur in all types of <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">stream</a> channels, from the smallest <a href="/wiki/Stream#Intermittent_and_ephemeral_streams" title="Stream">ephemeral streams</a> in humid zones to <a href="/wiki/Arroyo_(creek)" title="Arroyo (creek)">normally-dry channels</a> in arid climates to the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">world's largest</a> rivers. When overland flow occurs on tilled fields, it can result in a <a href="/wiki/Muddy_flood" title="Muddy flood">muddy flood</a> where <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a> are <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">picked up by run off</a> and carried as suspended matter or <a href="/wiki/Bed_load" title="Bed load">bed load</a>. Localized flooding may be caused or exacerbated by drainage obstructions such as <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_jam" title="Ice jam">ice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">debris</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Beaver" title="Beaver">beaver</a> dams.
</p><p>Slow-rising floods most commonly occur in large rivers with large <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">catchment areas</a>. The increase in flow may be the result of sustained rainfall, rapid snow melt, <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoons</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclones" title="Tropical cyclones">tropical cyclones</a>. However, large rivers may have rapid flooding events in areas with dry climate, since they may have large basins but small river channels and rainfall can be very intense in smaller areas of those basins.
</p><p>Rapid flooding events, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flash_floods" title="Flash floods">flash floods</a>, more often occur on smaller rivers, rivers with steep valleys, rivers that flow for much of their length over impermeable terrain, or normally-dry channels. The cause may be localized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convective_precipitation" title="Convective precipitation">convective precipitation</a> (intense <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a>) or sudden release from an upstream impoundment created behind a <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dam</a>, landslide, or <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glacier</a>. In one instance, a flash flood killed eight people enjoying the water on a Sunday afternoon at a popular waterfall in a narrow canyon. Without any observed rainfall, the flow rate increased from about 50 to 1,500 cubic feet per second (1.4 to 42 m<sup>3</sup>/s) in just one minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hjalmarson_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hjalmarson-7">[7]</a></sup> Two larger floods occurred at the same site within a week, but no one was at the waterfall on those days. The deadly flood resulted from a thunderstorm over part of the drainage basin, where steep, bare rock slopes are common and the thin soil was already saturated.
</p><p>Flash floods are the most common flood type in normally-dry channels in arid zones, known as <a href="/wiki/Arroyo_(creek)" title="Arroyo (creek)">arroyos</a> in the southwest United States and many other names elsewhere. In that setting, the first flood water to arrive is depleted as it wets the sandy stream bed. The leading edge of the flood thus advances more slowly than later and higher flows. As a result, the rising limb of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrograph" title="Hydrograph">hydrograph</a> becomes ever quicker as the flood moves downstream, until the flow rate is so great that the depletion by wetting soil becomes insignificant.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Estuarine_and_coastal">Estuarine and coastal</span></h3>
<p>Flooding in <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a> is commonly caused by a combination of storm surges caused by <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">winds</a> and low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barometric_pressure" title="Barometric pressure">barometric pressure</a> and large waves meeting high upstream river flows.
</p><p>Coastal areas may be flooded by storm surges combining with high tides and large wave events at sea, resulting in waves over-topping flood defenses or in severe cases by <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a> or tropical cyclones. A <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surge</a>, from either a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclone</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">extratropical cyclone</a>, falls within this category. Research from the NHC (National Hurricane Center) explains: "Storm surge is an additional rise of water generated by a storm, over and above the predicted astronomical tides. <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">Storm surge</a> should not be confused with storm tide, which is defined as the water level rise due to the combination of storm surge and the astronomical tide. This rise in water level can cause extreme flooding in coastal areas particularly when storm surge coincides with spring tide, resulting in storm tides reaching up to 20 feet or more in some cases."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_flooding">Urban flooding</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flooding_on_Water_Street_from_1881_-_DPLA_-_d52a2e7c08bea45238e206e7545b4133.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="502" data-file-width="468" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Flooding_on_Water_Street_from_1881_-_DPLA_-_d52a2e7c08bea45238e206e7545b4133.jpg/220px-Flooding_on_Water_Street_from_1881_-_DPLA_-_d52a2e7c08bea45238e206e7545b4133.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Flooding_on_Water_Street_from_1881_-_DPLA_-_d52a2e7c08bea45238e206e7545b4133.jpg/330px-Flooding_on_Water_Street_from_1881_-_DPLA_-_d52a2e7c08bea45238e206e7545b4133.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Flooding_on_Water_Street_from_1881_-_DPLA_-_d52a2e7c08bea45238e206e7545b4133.jpg/440px-Flooding_on_Water_Street_from_1881_-_DPLA_-_d52a2e7c08bea45238e206e7545b4133.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flooding_on_Water_Street_from_1881_-_DPLA_-_d52a2e7c08bea45238e206e7545b4133.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flooding on Water Street in Toledo, Ohio, 1881</div></div></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Urban_flooding" title="Urban flooding">Urban flooding</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Urban_flooding&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p><a href="/wiki/Urban_flooding" title="Urban flooding">Urban flooding</a> is the inundation of land or property in a <a href="/wiki/Built_environment" title="Built environment">built environment</a>, particularly in more densely populated areas, caused by rainfall overwhelming the capacity of drainage systems, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storm_sewers" title="Storm sewers">storm sewers</a>. Although sometimes triggered by events such as flash flooding or <a href="/wiki/Snowmelt" title="Snowmelt">snowmelt</a>, urban flooding is a condition, characterized by its repetitive and systemic impacts on communities, that can happen regardless of whether or not affected communities are located within designated floodplains or near any body of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urban_flooding_cnt_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urban_flooding_cnt-9">[9]</a></sup> Aside from potential overflow of rivers and lakes, snowmelt, <a href="/wiki/Stormwater" title="Stormwater">stormwater</a> or water released from damaged <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_network#Water_distribution_network" title="Water supply network">water mains</a> may accumulate on property and in public rights-of-way, seep through building walls and floors, or backup into buildings through sewer pipes, toilets and sinks.
</p><p>In urban areas, flood effects can be exacerbated by existing paved streets and roads, which increase the speed of flowing water. <a href="/wiki/Impervious_surface" title="Impervious surface">Impervious surfaces</a> prevent rainfall from infiltrating into the ground, thereby causing a higher surface run-off that may be in excess of local drainage capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
The flood flow in urbanized areas constitutes a hazard to both the population and infrastructure. Some recent catastrophes include the inundations of <a href="/wiki/N%C3%AEmes" title="Nîmes">Nîmes</a> (France) in 1998 and <a href="/wiki/Vaison-la-Romaine" title="Vaison-la-Romaine">Vaison-la-Romaine</a> (France) in 1992, the flooding of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> (USA) in 2005, and the flooding in <a href="/wiki/Rockhampton" title="Rockhampton">Rockhampton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bundaberg" title="Bundaberg">Bundaberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a> during the 2010–2011 summer in <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a> (Australia). Flood flows in urban environments have been studied relatively recently despite many centuries of flood events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urban_flooding_Chanson_2011c_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urban_flooding_Chanson_2011c-11">[11]</a></sup> Some recent research has considered the criteria for safe evacuation of individuals in flooded areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urban_flooding_Chanson_2014_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urban_flooding_Chanson_2014-12">[12]</a></sup></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catastrophic">Catastrophic</span></h3>
<p>Catastrophic riverine flooding is usually associated with major infrastructure failures such as the collapse of a dam, but they may also be caused by drainage channel modification from a <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">landslide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquake</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_eruption" title="Volcanic eruption">volcanic eruption</a>. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Outburst_flood" title="Outburst flood">outburst floods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lahar" title="Lahar">lahars</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">Tsunamis</a> can cause catastrophic <a href="/wiki/Coastal_flooding" title="Coastal flooding">coastal flooding</a>, most commonly resulting from undersea earthquakes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyclone_Hudhud_destruction_in_Visakhapatnam_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="722" data-file-width="968" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Cyclone_Hudhud_destruction_in_Visakhapatnam_2.jpg/220px-Cyclone_Hudhud_destruction_in_Visakhapatnam_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Cyclone_Hudhud_destruction_in_Visakhapatnam_2.jpg/330px-Cyclone_Hudhud_destruction_in_Visakhapatnam_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Cyclone_Hudhud_destruction_in_Visakhapatnam_2.jpg/440px-Cyclone_Hudhud_destruction_in_Visakhapatnam_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyclone_Hudhud_destruction_in_Visakhapatnam_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flood due to <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Hudhud" title="Cyclone Hudhud">Cyclone Hudhud</a> in <a href="/wiki/Visakhapatnam" title="Visakhapatnam">Visakhapatnam</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upslope_factors">Upslope factors</span></h3>
<p>The amount, location, and timing of water reaching a drainage channel from natural precipitation and controlled or uncontrolled reservoir releases determines the flow at downstream locations.  Some precipitation evaporates, some slowly percolates through soil, some may be temporarily sequestered as snow or ice, and some may produce rapid runoff from surfaces including rock, pavement, roofs, and saturated or frozen ground.  The fraction of incident precipitation promptly reaching a drainage channel has been observed from nil for light rain on dry, level ground to as high as 170 percent for warm rain on accumulated snow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most precipitation records are based on a measured depth of water received within a fixed time interval.  <i>Frequency</i> of a precipitation threshold of interest may be determined from the number of measurements exceeding that threshold value within the total time period for which observations are available.  Individual data points are converted to <i>intensity</i> by dividing each measured depth by the period of time between observations. This intensity will be less than the actual peak intensity if the <i>duration</i> of the rainfall event was less than the fixed time interval for which measurements are reported.  Convective precipitation events (thunderstorms) tend to produce shorter duration storm events than orographic precipitation. Duration, intensity, and frequency of rainfall events are important to flood prediction.  Short duration precipitation is more significant to flooding within small drainage basins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most important upslope factor in determining flood magnitude is the land area of the watershed upstream of the area of interest.  Rainfall intensity is the second most important factor for watersheds of less than approximately 30 square miles or 80 square kilometres.  The main channel slope is the second most important factor for larger watersheds.  Channel slope and rainfall intensity become the third most important factors for small and large watersheds, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simon-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_of_Concentration" title="Time of Concentration">Time of Concentration</a> is the time required for runoff from the most distant point of the upstream drainage area to reach the point of the drainage channel controlling flooding of the area of interest. The time of concentration defines the critical duration of peak rainfall for the area of interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urquhart_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urquhart-16">[16]</a></sup> The critical duration of intense rainfall might be only a few minutes for roof and parking lot drainage structures, while cumulative rainfall over several days would be critical for river basins.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Downslope_factors">Downslope factors</span></h3>
<p>Water flowing downhill ultimately encounters downstream conditions slowing movement. The final limitation in coastal flooding lands is often the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a> or some coastal flooding bars which form natural <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>. In flooding low lands, elevation changes such as tidal fluctuations are significant determinants of coastal and estuarine flooding. Less predictable events like tsunamis and storm surges may also cause elevation changes in large bodies of water. Elevation of flowing water is controlled by the geometry of the flow channel and, especially, by depth of channel, speed of flow and amount of sediments in it<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simon-15">[15]</a></sup>  Flow channel restrictions like bridges and canyons tend to control water elevation above the restriction. The actual control point for any given reach of the drainage may change with changing water elevation, so a closer point may control for lower water levels until a more distant point controls at higher water levels.
</p><p>Effective flood channel geometry may be changed by growth of vegetation, accumulation of ice or debris, or construction of bridges, buildings, or levees within the flood channel.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coincidence">Coincidence</span></h3>
<p>Extreme flood events often result from coincidence such as unusually intense, warm rainfall melting heavy snow pack, producing channel obstructions from floating ice, and releasing small impoundments like <a href="/wiki/Beaver" title="Beaver">beaver</a> dams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbett_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbett-17">[17]</a></sup> Coincident events may cause extensive flooding to be more frequent than anticipated from <a href="/wiki/100-year_flood" title="100-year flood">simplistic statistical prediction models</a> considering only precipitation runoff flowing within unobstructed drainage channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BR_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-BR-18">[18]</a></sup> Debris modification of channel geometry is common when heavy flows move uprooted woody vegetation and flood-damaged structures and vehicles, including boats and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railway" title="Railway">railway</a> equipment. Recent field measurements during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010%E2%80%9311_Queensland_floods" title="2010–11 Queensland floods">2010–11 Queensland floods</a> showed that any criterion solely based upon the flow velocity, water depth or specific momentum cannot account for the hazards caused by velocity and water depth fluctuations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chanson_2011c_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chanson_2011c-19">[19]</a></sup> These considerations ignore further the risks associated with large debris entrained by the flow motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chanson_2014_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chanson_2014-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some researchers have mentioned the storage effect in urban areas with transportation corridors created by <a href="/wiki/Cut_and_fill" title="Cut and fill">cut and fill</a>. Culverted fills may be converted to impoundments if the <a href="/wiki/Culvert" title="Culvert">culverts</a> become blocked by debris, and flow may be diverted along streets.  Several studies have looked into the flow patterns and redistribution in streets during storm events and the implication on flood modelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change#Floods" title="Effects of climate change">Effects of climate change § Floods</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Effects_of_climate_change&amp;action=edit#Floods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:4207Typhoons_Krosa_Lekima_%26_monsoon_tidal_flooding_in_Calumpit,_Bulacan_19.jpg"><img alt="A large flat sheet of water reflects a grey sky with green tropical vegetation in the background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/4207Typhoons_Krosa_Lekima_%26_monsoon_tidal_flooding_in_Calumpit%2C_Bulacan_19.jpg/300px-4207Typhoons_Krosa_Lekima_%26_monsoon_tidal_flooding_in_Calumpit%2C_Bulacan_19.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/4207Typhoons_Krosa_Lekima_%26_monsoon_tidal_flooding_in_Calumpit%2C_Bulacan_19.jpg/450px-4207Typhoons_Krosa_Lekima_%26_monsoon_tidal_flooding_in_Calumpit%2C_Bulacan_19.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/4207Typhoons_Krosa_Lekima_%26_monsoon_tidal_flooding_in_Calumpit%2C_Bulacan_19.jpg/600px-4207Typhoons_Krosa_Lekima_%26_monsoon_tidal_flooding_in_Calumpit%2C_Bulacan_19.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:4207Typhoons_Krosa_Lekima_%26_monsoon_tidal_flooding_in_Calumpit,_Bulacan_19.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tidal_flooding" title="Tidal flooding">High tides flooding</a> is increasing due to sea level rise, land subsidence, and the loss of natural barriers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flooding_in_the_US_Midwest_June_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Flooding_in_the_US_Midwest_June_2008.jpg/260px-Flooding_in_the_US_Midwest_June_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Flooding_in_the_US_Midwest_June_2008.jpg/390px-Flooding_in_the_US_Midwest_June_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Flooding_in_the_US_Midwest_June_2008.jpg/520px-Flooding_in_the_US_Midwest_June_2008.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flooding_in_the_US_Midwest_June_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flooding in the <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwestern United States</a>, June 2008</div></div></div>A warming climate will intensify rainfall events. When floods occur in this warmer future, these floods will be more severe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effects_of_climate_change_:18_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_:18-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1156">: 1156 </span></sup> Some regions will experience an increased in flooding, some a decrease. This depends on several factors, such as changes in snowmelt, soil moisture and rainfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effects_of_climate_change_:18_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_:18-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1156">: 1156 </span></sup> Flooding can be in the form of <a href="/wiki/Urban_flooding" title="Urban flooding">urban flooding</a>, river flooding or <a href="/wiki/Coastal_flooding" title="Coastal flooding">coastal flooding</a>, Sea level rise further increases risks of coastal flooding: if sea levels rise by a further 0.15 m, 20% more people will be exposed to a 1 in a 100 year coastal flood, assuming no population growth and no further adaptation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effects_of_climate_change_:20_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_:20-24">[24]</a></sup> With an extra 0.75 m, this rises to a doubling of people exposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effects_of_climate_change_:20_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Effects_of_climate_change_:20-24">[24]</a></sup>
Global warming makes bigger storm events more common due to an <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_water_cycle" title="Effects of climate change on the water cycle">intensification of the water cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This increase in the frequency of large storm events would alter existing <a href="/wiki/Intensity-duration-frequency_curve" title="Intensity-duration-frequency curve">Intensity-Duration-Frequency curves (IDF curves)</a> due to the change in frequency, but also by lifting and steepening the curves in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span></h2>
<p>Floods can also be a huge destructive power. When water flows, it has the ability to demolish all kinds of buildings and objects, such as  bridges, structures, houses, trees, cars...  For example, in Bangladesh in 2007, a flood was responsible for the destruction of more than one million houses. And yearly in the United States, floods cause over $7 billion in damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_effects">Primary effects</span></h3>
<p>The primary effects of flooding include <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">loss of life</a> and damage to buildings and other structures, including bridges, <a href="/wiki/Sewerage" title="Sewerage">sewerage</a> systems, roadways, and canals.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2foot_mud.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3881" data-file-width="5821" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/2foot_mud.jpg/220px-2foot_mud.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/2foot_mud.jpg/330px-2foot_mud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/2foot_mud.jpg/440px-2foot_mud.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2foot_mud.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A dog sitting on top of 2 feet of mud deposited by flooding in the <a href="/wiki/2018_Kerala_floods" title="2018 Kerala floods">2018 Kerala floods</a> in India. Flooding not only creates water damage, but can also deposit large amounts of sediment.</div></div></div>
<p>Floods also frequently damage <a href="/wiki/Power_transmission" title="Power transmission">power transmission</a> and sometimes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_generation" title="Power generation">power generation</a>, which then has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knock-on_effect" title="Knock-on effect">knock-on effects</a> caused by the loss of power. This includes loss of drinking <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">water treatment</a> and water supply, which may result in loss of drinking water or severe water contamination. It may also cause the loss of sewage disposal facilities. Lack of clean water combined with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_sewage" title="Human sewage">human sewage</a> in the flood waters raises the risk of <a href="/wiki/Waterborne_diseases" title="Waterborne diseases">waterborne diseases</a>, which can include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giardia" title="Giardia">giardia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryptosporidium" title="Cryptosporidium">cryptosporidium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> and many other diseases depending upon the location of the flood.
</p><p>Damage to roads and transport infrastructure may make it difficult to mobilize aid to those affected or to provide emergency health treatment.
</p><p>Flood waters typically inundate farm land, making the land unworkable and preventing crops from being planted or harvested, which can lead to shortages of food both for humans and farm animals. Entire harvests for a country can be lost in extreme flood circumstances. Some tree species may not survive prolonged flooding of their root systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span></h3>
<p>Fatalities connected directly to floods are usually caused by <a href="/wiki/Drowning" title="Drowning">drowning</a>; the waters in a flood are very deep and have strong <a href="/wiki/Current_(fluid)" title="Current (fluid)">currents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alderman_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-alderman-29">[29]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deaths" title="Deaths">Deaths</a> do not just occur from drowning, deaths are connected with <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heat_stroke" title="Heat stroke">heat stroke</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_attack" title="Heart attack">heart attack</a> and any other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illness" title="Illness">illness</a> that needs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_supplies" title="Medical supplies">medical supplies</a> that cannot be delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alderman_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-alderman-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Injuries can lead to an excessive amount of morbidity when a flood occurs. Injuries are not isolated to just those who were directly in the flood, <a href="/wiki/Rescue" title="Rescue">rescue</a> teams and even people delivering supplies can sustain an injury. Injuries can occur anytime during the flood process; before, during and after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alderman_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-alderman-29">[29]</a></sup> During floods accidents occur with falling <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">debris</a> or any of the many fast moving objects in the water. After the flood rescue attempts are where large numbers injuries can occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alderman_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-alderman-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communicable_diseases" title="Communicable diseases">Communicable diseases</a> are increased due to many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathogens" title="Pathogens">pathogens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> that are being transported by the <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.There are many <a href="/wiki/Waterborne_diseases" title="Waterborne diseases">waterborne diseases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">hepatitis A</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_E" title="Hepatitis E">hepatitis E</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diarrheal_diseases" title="Diarrheal diseases">diarrheal diseases</a>, to mention a few. <a href="/wiki/Gastrointestinal_disease" title="Gastrointestinal disease">Gastrointestinal disease</a> and diarrheal diseases are very common due to a lack of clean water during a flood. Most of clean water supplies are contaminated when flooding occurs. Hepatitis A and E are common because of the lack of <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> in the water and in living quarters depending on where the flood is and how prepared the <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a> is for a flood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alderman_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-alderman-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FEMA_-_37590_-_Community_Flooding_in_Florida.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1993" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/FEMA_-_37590_-_Community_Flooding_in_Florida.jpg/300px-FEMA_-_37590_-_Community_Flooding_in_Florida.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/FEMA_-_37590_-_Community_Flooding_in_Florida.jpg/450px-FEMA_-_37590_-_Community_Flooding_in_Florida.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/FEMA_-_37590_-_Community_Flooding_in_Florida.jpg/600px-FEMA_-_37590_-_Community_Flooding_in_Florida.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FEMA_-_37590_-_Community_Flooding_in_Florida.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coastal flooding in a Florida community.</div></div></div>
<p>Urban flooding can cause chronically wet houses, leading to the growth of <a href="/wiki/Indoor_mold" title="Indoor mold">indoor mold</a> and resulting in adverse health effects, particularly respiratory symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_diseases" title="Respiratory diseases">Respiratory diseases</a> are a common after the disaster has occurred. This depends on the amount of <a href="/wiki/Water_damage" title="Water damage">water damage</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mold_(fungus)" title="Mold (fungus)">mold</a> that grows after an incident. Research suggests that there will be an increase of 30-50% in adverse respiratory health outcomes caused by dampness and mold exposure for those living in coastal and wetland areas. Fungal contamination in homes is associated with increased allergic rhinitis and asthma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-31">[31]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">Vector</a> borne diseases increase as well due to the increase in still water after the floods have settled. The diseases that are vector borne are  <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dengue" title="Dengue">dengue</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">West Nile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alderman_29-5"><a href="#cite_note-alderman-29">[29]</a></sup> Floods have a huge impact on victims' psychosocial <a href="/wiki/Integrity" title="Integrity">integrity</a>. People suffer from a wide variety of losses and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(psychological)" title="Stress (psychological)">stress</a>. One of the most treated illness in long-term health problems are <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a> caused by the flood and all the <a href="/wiki/Tragedy" title="Tragedy">tragedy</a> that flows with one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alderman_29-6"><a href="#cite_note-alderman-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
